The second law of infodynamics and its implications for the simulated universe hypothesis featured

The simulation hypothesis is a philosophical theory, in which the entire universe and our objective reality are just simulated constructs. Despite the lack of evidence, this idea is gaining traction in scientific circles as well as in the entertainment industry. Recent scientific developments in the field of information physics, such as the publication of the mass-energy-information equivalence principle, appear to support this possibility. In particular, the 2022 discovery of the second law of information dynamics (infodynamics) facilitates new and interesting research tools at the intersection between physics and information. In this article, we re-examine the second law of infodynamics and its applicability to digital information, genetic information, atomic physics, mathematical symmetries, and cosmology, and we provide scientific evidence that appears to underpin the simulated universe hypothesis.

Topics
Laws of thermodynamics, General relativity, Information theory entropy, Electronic configuration, Nucleotides
I. INTRODUCTION
In 2022, a new fundamental law of physics has been proposed and demonstrated, called the second law of information dynamics, or simply the second law of infodynamics.1 Its name is an analogy to the second law of thermodynamics, which describes the time evolution of the physical entropy of an isolated system, which requires the entropy to remain constant or to increase over time. In contrast to the second law of thermodynamics, the second law of infodynamics states that the information entropy of systems containing information states must remain constant or decrease over time, reaching a certain minimum value at equilibrium. This surprising observation has massive implications for all branches of science and technology. With the ever-increasing importance of information systems such as digital information storage or biological information stored in DNA/RNA genetic sequences, this new powerful physics law offers an additional tool for examining these systems and their time evolution.2 

It is important to clearly distinguish between physical entropy and information entropy. The physical entropy of a given system is a measure of all its possible physical microstates compatible with the macrostate, SPhys. This is a characteristic of the non-information bearing microstates within the system. Assuming the same system, and assuming that one is able to create N information states within the same physical system (for example, by writing digital bits in it), the effect of creating a number of N information states is to form N additional information microstates superimposed onto the existing physical microstates. These additional microstates are information bearing states, and the additional entropy associated with them is called the entropy of information, SInfo. We can now define the total entropy of the system as the sum of the initial physical entropy and the newly created entropy of information, Stot = SPhys + SInfo, showing that the information creation increases the entropy of a given system. It is also important to clarify that information state is defined as any physical state, process, or event that can contain information in Shannonâs information theory framework.3 When a set of n independent and distinctive information states are created, X = {x1, x2, â¦, xn}, having a discrete probability distribution P = {p1, p2, â¦, pn}, the average information content per state is given by the Shannon information entropy formula3 
H(X)=ânj=1pjâlogb1pj.
(1)
The base of the logarithm, b, gives the units of information. When b = 2, the function H(X) returns an information value in bits. The function H(X) is maximum when the events xj have equal probabilities of occurring, pj = 1/n.
The reader should not confuse the Shannon information entropy H(X), with the entropy of the information bearing states, SInfo. Although the two parameters are closely linked, they are rather different quantities. If N information states are created within a given system containing n independent and distinctive information states, N â¥ n, then the additional possible states, also known as distinct messages in Shannonâs original formalism, are equivalent to the number of information bearing microstates, Î© compatible with the macrostate4,
Î©=nNâH(X).
(2)
The general entropy of the information bearing states is now derived as follows:
SInfo=Nâkbâlnnâânj=1pjâlogb1pj
(3)
or SInfo = NâÂ·âkbâÂ·âlnânâÂ·âH(X), where kb = 1.380â64 Ã 10â23 J/K is the Boltzmann constant. The second law of infodynamics states that1 
âSInfoâtâ¤0.
(4)
Since kb is a constant and n is the number of distinct events (information states), which is also a constant of the system, the decrease in the entropy of the information states can only come from the reduction over time in the total number of states, N, or a reduction over time in the Shannon entropy due to changes to the probabilities pj.
In what follows, we will examine a few diverse applications of the second law of infodynamics and demonstrate the universal nature of this new physics law, including the fact that it points to the characteristics of a computational system, underpinning to some degree the simulated universe hypothesis. A digital data storage system contains digital information states, having two distinct states, X={0,1}ï¿½=0,1â , so n = 2 and probabilities p={p0,p1}ï¿½=ï¿½0,ï¿½1â . The base in relation (1) is taken as b = 2 for units of digital bits. Assuming the system contains N bits, according to (2), it will have a total number of possible microstates,

Î©=2NâH(X).Î©=2ï¿½âï¿½(ï¿½).

(5)

The entropy of the information bearing states for a digital information system is

SInfo=Nâkbâln2ââ2j=1pjâlog21pj.ï¿½Info=ï¿½âï¿½ï¿½âln2ââï¿½=12ï¿½ï¿½âlog21ï¿½ï¿½.

The maximum Shannon entropy of this system is H(X) = 1, and it can deviate slightly from this upper limit, but this value is stable over time. Hence, in the case of digital information, the only parameter that can drive the time evolution of the entropy of the information bearing states is the total number of states, N. If N increases, then the information entropy increases. However, there is no mechanism that would result in spontaneous information being created without external intervention (i.e., energy input). In a previous study, we demonstrated that the only possible evolution of N over time is down or constant,1 in accordance with the second law of infodynamics. This is a very straightforward process and a direct consequence of the second law of thermodynamics because, over time, the digital states are eroded by thermal fluctuations, leading to the self-erasure of data. The higher the temperature of the environment, the more probable the data self-erasure processes are. Hence, in the case of digital information, the second law of infodynamics is rather trivial and fully expected. In our previous study, we demonstrated this using room temperature (300 K) micromagnetics modeling5 of a granular magnetic thin film structure with perpendicular uniaxial anisotropy of Ka = 8.75 Ã 106 J/m3 and Ms = 1710 kA/m. Figure 1 shows the schematic of the word INFORMATION written digitally onto a 400 Ã 550 Ã 2 nm3 magnetic thin film structure, resulting in a bit size of 50 Ã 50 nm2, which was allowed to evolve over time at room temperature.ChatGPTThe section delves into the application of the second law of infodynamics with respect to digital information storage.Key Points:Digital Information States: In a digital data storage system, there are only two distinct states â "0" and "1". Even though it might slightly deviate from this, this value remains relatively stable over time.Impact on Digital Information: Unlike physical systems where spontaneous creation or changes can occur, digital systems don't spontaneously create information. The number of bits, N, can either decrease or remain constant over time. This decrease is influenced by factors such as thermal fluctuations which can lead to data loss or "self-erasure". Essentially, as time goes on, external influences can cause stored digital data to degrade, especially at higher temperatures.Practical Demonstration: The article cites a prior study which demonstrated this principle using micromagnetic modeling of a granular magnetic thin film. A digital representation of the word "INFORMATION" was imprinted onto this film and was allowed to evolve over time at room temperature. The practical demonstration using micromagnetics further illustrates this phenomenon in real-world scenarios. SECOND LAW OF INFODYNAMICS AND GENETIC INFORMATION
A very interesting information storage system is a DNA/RNA sequence encoding biological information. This can be represented as a long string of the letters A, C, G, and T, where the characters represent adenine (A), cytosine (C), guanine (G), and thymine (T) [replaced with uracil (U) in RNA sequences]. Therefore, within Shannonâs information theory framework, a typical genome sequence can be represented as a probabilistic system of four distinctive states, n = 4, X={A,C,G,T}
 and probabilities p={pA,pC,pG,pT}
â . Using digital information units and Eq. (1), we determine that the maximum Shannon information entropy is H(X) = 2, and each nucleotide can encode a maximum of 2 bits: A = 00, C = 01, G = 10, T = 11. For a given genomic sequence containing N nucleotides, the total number of possible microstates is
Î©=4NâH(X).
(7)
The entropy of the information bearing states of a genomic sequence is
SInfo=Nâkbâln4ââ4j=1pjâlog21pj.
(8)
The time evolution of the entropy of genetic DNA/RNA information systems is given by the time evolution of the changes in their nucleotide sequence, called genetic mutations. Genetic mutations can take place via three mechanisms: (i) Single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), where changes occur so that the number of nucleotides N remains constant; (ii) deletions, where N decreases; and (iii) insertions, where N is increasingChatGPTThe section discusses the application of the second law of infodynamics to genetic information storage, specifically DNA/RNA sequences.Key Points:Genetic Information Storage: DNA and RNA sequences serve as nature's primary mechanism for storing biological information. This equation quantifies the "disorder" or uncertainty associated with a specific genomic sequence.Genetic Mutations & Entropy Evolution: The change in entropy for genetic systems over time correlates with genetic mutations. Three primary mutation mechanisms are:Single Nucleotide Polymorphisms (SNPs): Changes at single nucleotide sites without changing the total number of nucleotides (NNN remains constant).Deletions: Result in a reduction in the number of nucleotides (NNN decreases).Insertions: Add to the number of nucleotides (NNN increases).In essence, this section emphasizes the parallel between genetic mutations and the infodynamics of digital systems. Both digital and genetic systems can lose information (entropy) over time. However, the process and implications are vastly different. In digital systems, loss of information is seen as data degradation, while in biological systems, genetic mutations can lead to evolution, adaptation, and sometimes diseases or genetic disorders.2 / 2Similar to the case of digital information, a reduction of N would most likely result in a reduction of the overall entropy of the information bearing states, so âdeletionâ mutations would automatically fulfill the second law of infodynamics. In our previous study, we examined real data from RNA sequences that underwent only SNP mutations, which maintained the value of the N constant, and the reduction in the information entropy came only from Shannonâs information entropy function.1,2 Our test RNA sequences were variants of the novel SARS-CoV-2 virus, which emerged in December 2019 resulting in the COVID-19 pandemic. The reference RNA sequence of the SARS-CoV-2, collected in Wuhan, China in December 2019 (MN908947),6 has 29â903 nucleotides, so N = 29â903. All analyzed variants had 29â903 nucleotides and have been collected and sequenced at a later time, after undergoing an incremental number of SNP mutations. Shannon information entropies of the reference sequence and of the variants were computed using relation (1) and previously developed software, GENIES.7,8

Remarkably, the results indicate a unique correlation between the information and the dynamics of the genetic mutations by showing that the Shannon information entropy, H(X), and the overall information entropy of the SARS-CoV-2 variants (SInfo) computed using Eq. (8) decrease linearly with the number of mutations and over time, i.e., because number of mutations increase over time (see Fig. 2). The corresponding code names of the genome variants extracted from the NCBI database9â14 and analyzed in this work are shown next to each data point in Fig. 2. This result not only confirms the universal validity of the second law of infodynamics but also points to a possible governing mechanism of genetic mutations,2 currently believed to be just random events. The observation of the information entropic force that governs genetic mutations is very powerful because it challenges the Darwinian view that genetic mutations are complete random events and could be used to develop predictive algorithms for genetic mutations before they occur.2 We should acknowledge that, while all analyzed SARS-Cov-2 variants showed a decrease in their information entropy as they underwent genetic mutations, the data points presented in Fig. 2 have been carefully selected to emphasize the linear trend.ChatGPTThis section discusses how the second law of infodynamics applies to genetic information, particularly focusing on RNA sequences, and presents findings from a study on the SARS-CoV-2 virus.Key Points:Reduction of NNN: Similar to digital information, if the number of nucleotides, NNN, decreases (due to deletions), there is likely a corresponding decrease in the overall information entropy. Naturally, we asked whether the same RNA system would display behavior consistent with the second law of infodynamics, when the SARS-CoV-2 variants suffered âadditionâ mutations, so the number of nucleotides N is no longer constant but becomes larger than 29â903, increasing the information entropy. Using the NCBI database, we searched all the sequenced SARS-CoV-2 variants from January 1 2020 to January 1 2022. We searched only complete sequences with no missing/undetermined nucleotides, and the result was a total of 4.48 Ã 106 sequences. When we restricted the results to only the sequences that had at least 29â903 nucleotides or more, then 48â450 sequences were identified. Unfortunately, only one suffered a mutation where the resultant number of nucleotides increased by 1â29â904. Hence, 98.92% of all mutations took place via âdeletion,â reducing the total number of nucleotides. Since only one genome out of 4.48 Ã 106 appeared to increase the number of nucleotides, this is statistically irrelevant. Hence, we concluded that, for this test case, genetic mutations appear to take place in a way that reduces their information entropy, mostly via a deletion mechanism or a SNP. This is fully consistent with the second law of infodynamics, as a deletion would automatically decrease the total information entropy, and the SNPs have been shown to take place in a way that the information entropy is again reduced due to a reduction in Shannonâs information entropy.

We would also like to quote the famous Spiegelmanâs experiment that took place in 1972.15 In this experiment, Spiegelman studied the evolution of a virus over 74 generations. The virus was kept isolated in ideal conditions to survive, and with each generation, the virus was sequenced. The initial virus had 4500 base points, and with each generation, the genome decreased consistently in size. After 74 generations, the virus evolved to only 218 base points, showing an interesting and unexplained reduction of its genome of over 95%. This aligns with the second law of infodynamics, which predicts the degradation or stability of information over time.IV. SECOND LAW OF INFODYNAMICS AND HUNDâS RULE
Electronic states in atoms are fully described by four principal quantum numbers: (a) the principal quantum number, n. This number determines the energy of a particular shell or orbit, and it takes non-zero positive integral values n = 1, 2, 3, 4, â¦ (b) the orbital angular momentum quantum number, â. This quantum number describes the subshell, and gives the total angular momentum of an electron due to its orbital motion. This quantum number takes integral values restricted to â = 0, 1, 2, â¦, n â 1. (c) The magnetic quantum number, mâ. This quantum number determines the component (projection) of the orbital angular momentum along a specific direction, usually the direction of an applied magnetic field. It takes integral values, and for a given value of â, it may have (2â + 1) possible values: mâ = â, â â 1, â â 2, â¦, 0, ââ, â¦, â(â â 1), ââ. (d) The spin quantum number s, and the secondary spin quantum number, ms. The spin quantum number s gives the eigenvalues of the spin angular momentum operator, and it is related to the fact that the electron has an intrinsic angular momentum called âspinâ or spin angular momentum, which results from the rotation of the electron around an internal axis. The spin quantum number takes the values s = n/2, where n is a positive integer, so that s = 0, 1/2, 1, 3/2, 2, â¦. The secondary quantum spin number ms determines the direction (i.e., projection) of the spin angular momentum along the direction of an applied magnetic field. The allowed values of ms are 2s + 1 values from âs to +s in steps of 1. For example, an electron has s = 1/2, so the allowed values of ms are â1/2 and +1/2.

However, in the case of multi-electron atoms, multiple electron arrangements are possible while fulfilling Pauliâs exclusion principle.

In order to determine the electron population of an atomic orbital corresponding to the ground state of a multi-electron atom, German physicist Friedrich Hund formulated in 1927 a set of rules17 derived from phenomenological observations. These are called Hundâs rules, and when used in conjunction with Pauliâs exclusion principle, they are useful in atomic physics to determine the electron population of atoms corresponding to the ground state.

To explain this, let us assume that an atom has three electrons on its p orbital. Figure 3 shows the three allowed ground state distinctive configurations that fulfill Pauliâs exclusion principle, resulting in total spin quantum values of 1/2, 3/2, and 1/2, respectively.ChatGPTThe given passage delves into the intricacies of quantum numbers, which are parameters defining the state of an electron in an atom, and how they relate to Hund's rules in determining electron configurations for multi-electron atoms.Key Takeaways:Quantum Numbers:Principal Quantum Number (n): Determines the energy of a particular shell or orbit in an atom and can take values such as 1, 2, 3, 4, â¦Orbital Angular Momentum Quantum Number (â): Describes the subshell of an electron and its total angular momentum. It can take values between 0 and n-1.Magnetic Quantum Number (mâ): Represents the component of the orbital angular momentum in a specific direction (e.g., direction of an applied magnetic field). The spin quantum number sss can be 0, 1/2, 1, 3/2, 2, â¦, while the secondary spin quantum number msmsms determines the projection of the spin angular momentum and can take values from âs-sâs to +s+s+s in increments of 1.Pauli's Exclusion Principle: A principle stating that no two electrons can have the same set of four quantum numbers, thus ensuring that electrons in a multi-electron atom occupy different quantum states.Hund's Rules: Formulated in 1927 by Friedrich Hund, these rules are guidelines to determine the ground state electron configuration of multi-electron atoms. These rules ensure the maximum multiplicity (i.e., electrons have parallel spins as much as possible) and the lowest possible energy.Example with p Orbital: For an atom with three electrons in its p orbital, there are three possible configurations that adhere to Pauliâs exclusion principle. Each configuration will have a unique set of total spin quantum values.In essence, understanding quantum numbers and Hundâs rules provides a foundational knowledge for predicting electron configurations in atoms, which in turn helps understand atomic properties and behavior. The configuration of electrons around an atom plays a crucial role in determining the atom's chemical properties and reactivity.Three electrons residing on a p orbital and their allowed arrangements according to Pauliâs exclusion principle.

The correct electronic arrangement is given by Hundâs first rule, which is the most important, and it is simply called Hundâs rule. This states that the lowest energy atomic state is the one that maximizes the total spin quantum number, meaning simply that the orbitals of the subshell are each occupied singly with electrons of parallel spin before double occupation occurs. Therefore, the term with the lowest energy is also the term with the maximum number of unpaired electrons, so for our example shown in Fig. 3, Hundâs rule dictates that the correct configuration is the middle one, resulting in a total spin quantum value of 3/2.

Hundâs rule is derived from empirical observations, and there is no clear understating of why the electrons populate atomic orbitals in this way. So far, two different physical explanations have been given in Ref. 18. Both explanations revolve around the energetic balance of the electrons and their interactions in the atom. The first mechanism implies that electrons in different orbitals are further apart, so that electronâelectron repulsion energy is reduced. The second mechanism claims that the electrons in singly occupied orbitals are less effectively screened from the nucleus, resulting in a contraction of the orbitals, which increases the electronânucleus attraction energy.19 

In this article, we examine the electronic population in atoms within the framework of information theory3 and we demonstrate that Hundâs rule (Hundâs first rule) is a direct consequence of the second law of information dynamics.1 This requires that, at equilibrium in the ground state, electrons occupy the orbitals in such a way that their information entropy is minimum, or equivalently, the bit information content per electron is minimum.

Since their n, â, and mâ values are the same, their ms must be different to fulfill Pauliâs exclusion principle.

The Ising model is a well-known mathematical model in statistical mechanics, originally introduced to explain ferromagnetism in certain materials. In this model, spins (often referred to as "spins up" and "spins down") are arranged on a lattice, and the model focuses on the interactions between neighboring spins. The synchronous update method (or "Ising synch") refers to updating all spins at the same time in each iteration, rather than one by one.Now, connecting the Ising model to the earlier concept of Shannon entropy and Hundâs rule:Spins in the Ising Model: Just like the electron spins, the Ising model spins can be either up (â) or down (â). Neighboring spins influence each other: in a ferromagnetic material, there is an energetic preference for neighboring spins to align in the same direction, while in an antiferromagnetic material, there's a preference for them to be opposite.Shannon Entropy in the Ising Model: If we consider each spin in the Ising model to be a random variable, we can calculate a Shannon entropy for the entire system. This entropy will be at its maximum when there is an equal probability for each spin to be up or down (maximum disorder). In contrast, if all spins are in the same direction (either all up or all down), the entropy is minimized, which reflects a perfectly ordered state.Relating to Hundâs Rule: In Hund's rule, electrons fill up orbitals in a way that maximizes the number of unpaired electrons with parallel spins. This can be likened to the Ising model preference for aligned neighboring spins in ferromagnetic materials. The state of maximum aligned spins would correspond to a state of minimum Shannon entropy in the context of the electron spin discussion we had earlier.Ising Synch and Hundâs Rule: By applying an Ising synchronous update, we're essentially making a parallel to the way electrons might "decide" their spins all at once to minimize the system's energy (akin to Hund's rule). In each iteration, spins might "choose" their alignment based on their neighbors in a way that reduces the overall system's energy. This iterative process might ultimately lead to a state where spins (or electron configurations in an atom) are arranged in a manner consistent with Hundâs rule.In essence, the Ising synch could be viewed as a model for understanding how a system might dynamically evolve towards a state of minimum entropy or energy, which can be likened to how electrons fill orbitals following Hund's rule.So it looks like in the experiment they added noise or entropy which degrades the information and eventually erases it?ChatGPTYes, from your initial description regarding the behavior of the SARS-CoV-2 virus and the 1972 Spiegelman's experiment, it seems that the "noise" or entropy in the system, in the form of random mutations, tends to degrade the information content of the genetic material. The so-called "second law of infodynamics" appears to be a fundamental principle that not only aligns with but also might explain the origins of Hundâs rule.In atomic physics, Hund's rules refers to a set of rules that German physicist Friedrich Hund formulated around 1925, which are used to determine the term symbol that corresponds to the ground state of a multi-electron atom. The first rule is especially important in chemistry, where it is often referred to simply as Hund's Rule.

The three rules are:[1][2][3]

For a given electron configuration, the term with maximum multiplicity has the lowest energy. The multiplicity is equal to

These rules specify in a simple way how usual energy interactions determine which term includes the ground state. The rules assume that the repulsion between the outer electrons is much greater than the spinâorbit interaction, which is in turn stronger than any other remaining interactions. This is referred to as the LS coupling regime.ChatGPTHund's rules play a significant role in determining the ground state of a multi-electron atom. These rules help predict the most stable electronic arrangement in an atom, particularly for atoms in the transition metal series where multiple configurations are possible.Let's summarize Hund's rules in a more concise form:Maximum Multiplicity (often called Hund's First Rule):The electron configuration with the greatest number of unpaired electrons (maximum total spin angular momentum, SSS) will have the lowest energy.Multiplicity = 2S+12S + 12S+1, so a greater SSS yields a higher multiplicity.Maximum Orbital Angular Momentum (Hund's Second Rule):Among configurations with the same multiplicity, the configuration with the largest total orbital angular momentum quantum number (LLL) is lowest in energy.Alignment of Total Angular Momentum (Hund's Third Rule):For a given term symbol (i.e., given SSS and LLL):If the outermost subshell is half-filled or less, the level with the smallest total angular momentum quantum number (JJJ) will be the lowest in energy.If the outermost subshell is more than half-filled, the level with the highest JJJ will have the lowest energy. Here, JJJ is derived from the vector sum of the spin (SSS) and orbital (LLL) angular momenta, represented as J=L+SJ = L + SJ=L+S.The logic behind Hund's rules is rooted in electron-electron repulsions and the balance between spin and spatial aspects of the electron distribution. Specifically, Hund's rules are most applicable in the LS coupling regime, where electron-electron repulsions dominate, followed in significance by the spin-orbit interaction, with other interactions being of even lower significance.Hund's rules provide guidelines for determining the term symbol, which represents the ground state of a multi-electron atom. 

The rules were formulated by Friedrich Hund in the mid-1920s and are widely used in chemistry and atomic physics.

Here's a breakdown of the three rules:

1. The term with the maximum multiplicity has the lowest energy. The multiplicity (2S+1) is determined by the total spin angular momentum (S) of all the electrons.

The multiplicity is also equal to the number of unpaired electrons plus one. Therefore, the term with the lowest energy is the one with the maximum S and the maximum number of unpaired electrons.

2. For a given multiplicity, the term with the largest value of the total orbital angular momentum quantum number (L) has the lowest energy. This rule takes into account the orbital angular momentum of the electrons.

3. For a given term, if the outermost subshell is half-filled or less, the level with the lowest value of the total angular momentum quantum number (J) is lowest in energy. However, if the outermost subshell is more than half-filled, the level with the highest value of J is lowest in energy. This rule considers the total angular momentum (J) of the electrons, which is the sum of the orbital angular momentum (L) and the spin angular momentum (S).

It's important to note that these rules assume certain conditions, such as the dominance of electron repulsion over spin-orbit interaction and the absence of other significant interactions. This corresponds to the LS coupling regime, where the total angular momentum (J) is a good quantum number.

Hund's rules provide a simple framework to understand the energy interactions in multi-electron atoms and determine the ground state term based on the electron configuration.ChatGPTThat's a clear and concise summary of Hund's rules. By following these guidelines, one can determine the most probable electron configuration for the ground state of a multi-electron atom, especially useful for the transition metals and rare earth elements where there are multiple possible configurations due to closely spaced energy levels.Understanding Hund's rules becomes especially crucial when diving deeper into atomic and molecular spectroscopy, where the term symbols associated with different electron configurations can explain observed spectra. Additionally, the rules are foundational in quantum chemistry and materials science, where understanding the electronic structure of atoms and molecules is essential for predicting properties and behaviors of substances.Before we finished reviewing the article on infodynamics I wanted to discuss black holes and admit a bias I had before reading the articleChatGPTOf course! Each theory brings its unique viewpoint and potential solutions to long-standing problems in black hole physics.V. SECOND LAW OF INFODYNAMICS IN COSMOLOGY
The universe can only be either finite/close or infinite/open. The current consensus is that we live in an infinite universe that is in continuous expansion. Regardless of whether the universe is finite or infinite, the thermodynamic laws are equally applicable. The first law of thermodynamics states that energy can neither be created nor destroyed; it is conserved. The energy in the universe can only be converted from one form to another, but overall, it remains constant. Using Clausiusâ sign convention, the mathematical differential form of the first law of thermodynamics is
dQâ=âdUâ+âdW,
(11)
where Q is the net heat energy supplied to the universe, W captures the work done by the universe in all possible forms, and U represents the total internal energy of matter and radiation in the universe.
However, the universe does not exchange heat with anything, so if the universe is expanding adiabatically, then the first law becomes
0=dQâ=âdUâ+âdW.
(12)
We now recall the relation that links heat to entropy, dQ = TâÂ·âdS, where S is the total entropy of the universe and T is the temperature. Since T has a non-zero value as dictated by the third law of thermodynamics, and the average temperature of the observable universe could, in fact, be considered to be 2.7 K, we deduce that dS = 0. This implies that the total entropy of the universe must be constant. This constant entropy does not violate the second law of thermodynamics, which allows the entropy to be constant over time or to increase. However, in an expanding universe, the entropy will always increase because more possible microstates are being created via the expansion of the space itself. Here's how it ties into black holes and the evolving universe:Hawking Radiation: Black holes are not completely "black." According to quantum mechanics, particle-antiparticle pairs spontaneously form and annihilate near the event horizon. Occasionally, one of these particles falls into the black hole while the other escapes. The escaping particle carries away energy, resulting in the black hole losing mass over time. This process is incredibly slow for large black holes but speeds up as they shrink.Temperature and Black Hole Evaporation: Black holes have a temperature inversely proportional to their mass. The smaller the black hole, the hotter it is. As a black hole emits Hawking radiation and loses mass, its temperature increases, which in turn increases the rate of radiation emission. This is a feedback loop that accelerates the evaporation process as the black hole gets smaller.Endgame in a Cold Universe: As you pointed out, trillions of years from now, the universe will likely have expanded so much that it cools down considerably. When the ambient temperature of the universe drops below the temperature of the black hole, the black hole will lose energy more rapidly to Hawking radiation than it gains from the cosmic background. This will cause the black hole to evaporate more quickly. Ultimately, as black holes radiate away their mass, they can eventually explode in a burst of energy when they get very small.Cosmic Implications: In a universe that's cold and expanded, the main sources of energy could be these evaporating black holes. But once the last of them evaporates, the universe will be left in a state of thermodynamic equilibrium, often referred to as "heat death," where it's uniform, cold, and without free energy to fuel processes.Your assertion is indeed a deep and essential concept when discussing the long-term fate of the universe and black holes. THE FUZZY YARNBALL THEORY: A NEW PERSPECTIVE ON BLACK HOLESThe classical description of black holes paints a picture of an infinitely dense point known as a singularity, shrouded by the event horizon, beyond which no information can escape. However, the Fuzzy Yarnball Theory (FYT) offers a novel and intriguing perspective on the inner workings of black holes.The Core: Instead of a singularity, FYT proposes that the core of a black hole is akin to a neutron star, a densely packed sphere of neutrons. These oscillations might lead to the release of energy or information in specific conditions.Event Horizon and Atmosphere: The event horizon of the black hole in the FYT is well above the core. VII. CONCLUSIONS
In this study, we revisited the second law of infodynamics, first introduced in 2022.1 The second law of infodynamics states that the information entropy of systems containing information states must remain constant or decrease over time, reaching a certain minimum value at equilibrium. This is very interesting because it is in total opposition to the second law of thermodynamics, which describes the time evolution of the physical entropy that must increase up to a maximum value at equilibrium.

We showed that the second law of infodynamics is universally applicable to any system containing information states, including biological systems and digital data. Remarkably, this indicates that the evolution of biological life tends in such a way that genetic mutations are not just random events as per the current Darwinian consensus, but instead undergo genetic mutations according to the second law of infodynamics, minimizing their information entropy. This discovery has massive implications for genetic research, evolutionary biology, genetic therapies, pharmacology, virology, and pandemic monitoring, to name a few.

Here, we also expanded the applicability of the second law of infodynamics to explain phenomenological observations in atomic physics. In particular, we demonstrated that the second law of infodynamics explains the rule followed by the electrons to populate the atomic orbitals in multi-electron atoms, known as the Hundâs rule. Electrons arrange themselves on orbitals, at equilibrium in the ground state, in such a way that their information entropy is always minimal.

Most interesting is the fact that the second law of infodynamics appears to be a cosmological necessity. Here, we re-derived this new physics law using thermodynamic considerations applied to an adiabatically expanding universe.

Finally, one of the great mysteries of nature is: Why does symmetry dominate in the universe? has also been explained using the second law of infodynamics. Using simple geometric shapes, we demonstrated that high symmetry always corresponds to the lowest information entropy state, or lowest information content, explaining why everything in nature tends to symmetry instead of asymmetry.

The key question is now: âWhat can we learn from the second law of infodynamics and what is its meaning?â

The second law of infodynamics essentially minimizes the information content associated with any event or process in the universe. The minimization of the information really means an optimisation of the information content, or the most effective data compression, as described in Shannonâs information theory. This behavior is fully reminiscent of the rules deployed in programming languages and computer coding. Since the second law of infodynamics appears to be manifesting universally and is, in fact, a cosmological necessity, we could conclude that this points to the fact that the entire universe appears to be a simulated construct. A super complex universe like ours, if it were a simulation, would require a built-in data optimization and compression mechanism in order to reduce the computational power and the data storage requirements. This is exactly what we are observing via empirical evidence all around us, including digital data, biological systems, atomistic systems, symmetries, and the entire universe.

Another important aspect of the second law of infodynamics is the fact that it appears to validate the mass-energy-information equivalence principle formulated in 2019.4 According to this principle, the information itself is not just a mathematical construct or just physical, as postulated by Landauer25 and experimentally demonstrated recently,26â29 but it has a small mass and can be regarded as the fifth form of matter.4 This principle has not been confirmed experimentally yet, and it has attracted a fair share of skepticism. Whether information is physical or not is irrelevant to this study because the second law of infodynamics is applicable regardless of whether information has mass or not. However, if information is physical (equivalent to mass and energy), then the second law of thermodynamics requires systems to evolve in such a way that the energy is minimized at equilibrium. Hence, a reduction in the information content, would translate into a reduction of mass-energy according to the mass-energy-information equivalence principle. Therefore, the second law of infodynamics is not just a cosmological necessity, but since it is required to fulfill the second law of thermodynamics, we can conclude that this new physics law proves that information is indeed physical. The scientific evidence supporting the simulated universe theory is also discussed in greater detail in a recently published book. 30
